Delegated Divorce with Payment: Al-Zafrani vs. Imam Muhammad
Intra-Hanafi dispute on delegated divorce with payment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute regarding the occurrence of second and third divorces in a delegated divorce with payment, and apply al-Zafrani's position to a similar scenario.

Core Idea
Only the first divorce falls with payment; the rest require renewal without payment because she becomes bain.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning a husband who delegates divorce to his consummated wife for a payment of 1,000 dirhams. She immediately pronounces all three divorces. The question is: how many divorces occur, and with what financial consequences? The passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents two positions: that of al-Zafrani (preferred) and the riwayah of Imam Muhammad. Understanding this dispute clarifies how delegation (tafwid) interacts with irrevocable divorce (bain) and compensation.

Guided Path
1
Establish the scenario
Identify the key facts: husband says 'Divorce yourself three times for the sunna for a thousand' to his consummated wife. She is pure and not in a state of intercourse. She immediately responds with the same phrase.
The phrase 'lil-sunna' indicates one divorce per tuhr, so the husband intends three separate divorces over three clean periods.
2
State al-Zafrani's position
According to al-Zafrani, only one divorce occurs immediately, with one-third of the payment (333.33 dirhams). The second and third divorces do not occur until she renews the pronouncement in subsequent tuhrs. If she renews, they occur without any additional payment because she is already bain and cannot contract compensation.
The rationale: delegation attached each divorce to a specific tuhr. The wife's single utterance only counts for the first tuhr.
3
State Imam Muhammad's position
In the riwayah of Muhammad (al-Shaybani), no divorces occur at all—neither with nor without compensation. Reason: the second and third cannot be with compensation because after the first divorce (if it fell) she becomes bain and can't own herself; but without compensation, the husband did not consent to free divorces.
Imam Muhammad's view leads to the husband having to initiate another divorce process from scratch.
4
Analyze the analogy (qiyas) for free divorces
Al-Zafrani supports his view with an analogy: if a husband orders his already-separated wife to divorce herself for compensation, and she does so, the divorce occurs for free (no compensation). Here, after the first divorce, she is bain, so the same rule applies to the subsequent delegated divorces.
The analogy emphasizes that delegation implies consent to the divorce even if compensation becomes impossible.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The author of al-Bahr al-Raiq (Ibn Nujaym) presents al-Zafrani's view as the preferred one. It is more practical and consistent with the principles of delegation. Thus, the operative ruling for fatwa is: first divorce falls with 1/3 payment; second and third require renewal and occur for free.
This is a later ikhtilaf; the Zahir al-Riwayah does not explicitly address this case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقع الثانية بثلث الألف، وكذا الثالثة على هذا الوجه .اهـ. وفي المحيط : قال للمدخولة طلقي نفسك ثلاثاً للسنة بألف فقالت طلقت نفسي ثلاثاً للسنة بألف ؛ فإن كانت طاهرة من غير جماع طلقت للحال واحدة ولا تقع الثانية والثالثة إلا بتجديد الإيقاع في مجلس السنة فيقعان بغير شيء. هكذا ذكر الزعفراني لأنه فوض إليها إيقاع كل تطليقة في كل طهر فيكون بمنزلة المضاف إلى وقت كل طهر لم يجامعها فيه فلا تملك إيقاعها حتى يجيء الوقت وقد أمرها بالإيقاع فلا بد من التجديد وإنما يقعان مجاناً لأنها بانت بالأولى فلا تملك نفسها بالثانية والثالثة ألا ترى أنه لو أمرها أن تطلق نفسها ببدل بعدما أبانها ففعلت وقع مجاناً، وفي رواية محمد لا يقع بهذا القول أبداً لأنه تعذر إيقاعهما بعوض لما بينا وتعذر إيقاعهما بغير عوض لأن الزوج لم يرض بوقوعهما مجاناً فلم يقعا .اهـ.
The second [divorce] occurs with one-third of the thousand, and likewise the third in this manner. In al-Muhit: He said to a consummated wife, 'Divorce yourself three times for the sunna for a thousand,' and she said, 'I divorce myself three times for the sunna for a thousand.' If she was pure without intercourse, one [divorce] occurs immediately, and the second and third do not occur except by renewing the pronouncement in the session of the sunna, and they occur without anything. This is what al-Zafrani mentioned because he delegated to her the pronouncing of each divorce in each tuhr, so it is like being attached to the time of each tuhr in which he did not have intercourse with her, so she does not have the power to pronounce it until the time comes, and he ordered her to pronounce so renewal is necessary, and they occur for free because she becomes separated by the first, so she does not own herself for the second and third. Do you not see that if he ordered her to divorce herself for compensation after he had separated her, and she did so, it would occur for free. In the riwayah of Muhammad, it never occurs by this statement because the pronouncement of both with compensation is impossible as we explained, and their pronouncement without compensation is impossible because the husband did not consent to their occurrence for free, so they do not occur.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a specific case of delegated divorce with compensation. The scenario: a husband tells his consummated wife to divorce herself three times for the sunna (meaning one divorce in each of three successive tuhrs) for a payment of 1,000 dirhams. She immediately says 'I divorce myself three times for the sunna for 1,000.' The ruling: according to al-Zafrani, if she is pure and not in a state of intercourse, one divorce occurs immediately with one-third of the payment. The second and third divorces do not occur until she pronounces them again in the subsequent tuhrs, and if she does, they occur without any additional payment because after the first divorce she becomes bain (separated) and no longer owns herself, so she cannot contract compensation. Al-Zafrani's reasoning is that the husband delegated the timing to each tuhr; the wife's single utterance is only effective for the first, and she must renew for the others. In the riwayah of Imam Muhammad, the statement never makes any of the divorces occur—neither with nor without compensation—because the pronouncement of all three with compensation is impossible (since the second and third cannot be with compensation after bain) and without compensation is not consented to by the husband. The mufta bihi position is al-Zafrani's, as it is more practical and aligns with the principle of delegation.

Key Terms
الزعفراني (al-Za'farānī): A Hanafi scholar known for transmitting rulings; his view is cited as authoritative here.
بائن (bā'in): An irrevocable divorce; the wife becomes forbidden to the husband without a new marriage contract.
تطليقة (taṭlīqah): The pronouncement of a divorce, counted as one of the three permissible divorces.
تفويض (tafwīḍ): Delegation of the right to divorce to the wife.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where a husband says to his consummated wife 'طلقي نفسك ثلاثاً للسنة بألف', and she immediately says 'طلقت نفسي ثلاثاً للسنة بألف' while she is pure and not touched, how many divorces occur according to al-Zafrani?
A) Three divorces immediately, each with one-third of the payment.
B) One divorce immediately with one-third of the payment, and the second and third occur only if she renews in the next tuhrs without payment.
C) No divorce occurs because the statement is invalid.
D) All three divorces occur but without any payment.

Key Takeaway
In delegated divorce with payment, the first divorce occurs with payment, and subsequent divorces require renewal in their respective tuhrs and occur without payment, according to the mufta bihi view of al-Zafrani.

Ruling for Non-Consummated Wife in Khul'
Specific khul' ruling for non-consummated wife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a non-consummated wife when the husband pronounces three divorces in exchange for a thousand, and calculate the apportionment of payment.

Core Idea
For a non-consummated wife, one divorce falls immediately with one-third payment; the rest are pending on remarriage.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq, the author explains a specific scenario in khul' (divorce for compensation). When a husband says to his non-consummated wife, 'You are divorced three times for the sunna for a thousand,' without specifying intention, Hanafi jurisprudence applies a unique apportionment rule. This lesson unpacks that rule and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The husband addresses a non-consummated wife with a triple divorce statement linked to a payment. No specific intention is mentioned. Understand that all times are suitable for sunna divorce for such a wife.
Non-consummated means no sexual intercourse has occurred.
2
Understand the apportionment
The payment (a thousand) is matched against three divorces. Since the divorce falls in one session (bidding for sunna), only one divorce occurs immediately, and the payment is divided equally: one-third.
This is based on the principle that the compensation is distributed proportionally.
3
Analyze the remaining divorces
The other two divorces do not fall immediately but are held in abeyance. If the husband remarries her, each subsequent marriage triggers one divorce with its one-third payment.
This is because the original pronouncement was valid but its effect is postponed until ownership is reestablished.
4
Compare with consummated wife
The passage contrasts with a consummated wife: divorces fall one per tuhr, and payment is only for the first divorce.
This highlights the difference in application based on marital status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

حتى يقبلوا للإجماع على أن بقبولها قوله : (مع أن والفعل بمعنى المصدر) قال الرملي أقول قال في الخانية في الفرق : لأن كلمة «على» لتعليق الايجاب بالقبول لا للتعليق بوجود المقبول ا.هـ. فيتعجب من كلامه وقد تبعه أخوه في ذلك والله تعالى هو الموفق تأمل ا هـ . قلت : لا يخفى عليك أنه لا عجب في كلامهما بل في كلامه لأن ما ذكره لا يصلح فرقاً بين على دخولك وعلى أن تدخلي، والفرق المذكور قد مر في كلام المؤلف عند In al-Muhit: If he said to a non-consummated wife, 'You are divorced three times for the sunna for a thousand or upon a thousand,' and he had no intention, she is divorced once with one-third of the thousand because all times with respect to a non-consummated woman are times for sunna divorce, and he has matched the thousand with the three, so it is divided among them. If he marries her a second time, she is divorced another with one-third, and likewise a third time, because the pronouncement was valid and does not cease with the termination of ownership; when ownership exists, the condition exists, so it falls and does not require new acceptance from her because acceptance is required in the session of address and it has occurred, except that the falling is delayed due to lack of place, like if he said 'You are divorced tomorrow for a thousand' and she accepted, then tomorrow came, she is divorced for a thousand without acceptance. If she was consummated, one falls in a tuhr in which he did not have intercourse with her with one-third of the thousand, then another in the second tuhr, and another in the third without anything because the compensation is owed in exchange for the ownership of marriage, which has ceased with the first, so she does not own herself for the second for compensation to be valid. If she accepted while she was in a state of menstruation, nothing falls until she menstruates and becomes pure, then it falls as we mentioned. Then know that divorce on property is a yamin from his side, so its idafa and ta'liq are valid, his revocation is not valid, and it is not invalidated by his standing up from the session, and it depends on reaching her if she is absent. From her side it is exchange (mubadala), so her ta'liq and idafa are not valid, and her revocation before the husband's acceptance is valid if she initiated, and it is invalidated by her standing up. An example is his statement 'I have a thousand upon you on condition that you give me a thousand' as opposed to 'if you give me' or 'if you answer me with a thousand' — then she is not divorced until she gives because of the explicit conditioning of giving, contrary to the use of 'ala' [upon condition]. Even if the husband had a debt to her, setoff occurs in the case of 'ala an tu'tini' [on condition that you give me] but not in 'in a'taytini' [if you give me] unless the husband consents to a future divorce for a thousand owed to him. This is because it is said 'ala an tu'tini kaza' and its acceptance is intended in custom. Allah said: 'until they give jizya out of hand while they are subdued' meaning until they accept. Based on consensus that by her acceptance... His statement: 'with 'an' and the verb being in the meaning of the verbal noun.' Al-Ramli said: I say that al-Khaniyya said in the distinction: because the word 'ala' is for conditioning the offer on acceptance, not for conditioning on the existence of the thing accepted. This is astonishing. His brother followed him in that. May Allah grant success. Reflect. I say: It is not hidden from you that there is no astonishment in their words, but rather in his, because what he mentioned does not suffice as a distinction between 'ala dukhulik' and 'ala an tadkhuli'. The distinction mentioned has already passed in the author's words at his statement 'and the money becomes binding on her' and he repeated it before his statement 'she said divorce me three for a thousand.' I have seen in the handwriting of some scholars, quoting al-Subki, what clarifies the distinction, Allah willing. Its text: 'The difference between the explicit verbal noun and 'an' + verb is that they share the indication of the event, but the subject of the explicit verbal noun is the event only, which is a conceptual matter, while 'an' + verb adds to that the occurrence — either past, present, or future if affirmative, and non-occurrence if negative — which is a verititive matter. This is why 'an'+verb can take the place of two objects because of the relationship between them.' End quote. Similar is in al-Ashbah al-Nahwiyyah. You have learned from what has passed that the word 'ala' is a condition and that divorce...
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage covers multiple topics. First, the scenario for a non-consummated wife: the husband says 'أنت طالق ثلاثاً للسنة بألف' without specifying intention. The ruling: one divorce occurs immediately with one-third of the thousand. The remaining two divorces occur if he remarries her and she becomes his wife again, because the original pronouncement was valid but its effect was postponed due to lack of ownership (since after the first divorce, she is not his wife). This is analogous to a suspended divorce ('أنت طالق غداً بألف'). For a consummated wife, one divorce falls in each tuhr, with payment only for the first. Then the author states general principles: divorce for property (khul') is a yamin (oath) from the husband's side, meaning his pronouncement is like a vow — he can add conditions (idafa, ta'liq), cannot revoke it, and it doesn't become void by leaving the session. From the wife's side, it is a financial exchange (mubadala) — she cannot suspend it or add conditions, and she may revoke before the husband's acceptance if she initiated. The text discusses the difference between 'على أن تعطيني' (on condition that you give) and 'إن أعطيتني' (if you give). In the former, acceptance is required; in the latter, it is a condition. The verse from Quran (9:29) is cited as proof for the use of 'عن يد' meaning 'by acceptance.' The final part is a grammatical-legal discussion about the difference between the مصدر صريح (explicit verbal noun) and 'أن + فعل', citing al-Subki. This distinction matters in understanding the difference between 'على دخولك' and 'على أن تدخلي' in khul' contracts. The author of al-Bahr al-Raiq (Ibn Nujaym) refutes a claim by al-Ramli and supports the earlier distinction given by the author.

Key Terms
الطلاق على مال (al-ṭalāq ʿalā māl): Divorce in exchange for compensation (khul'); the wife pays the husband to obtain her release.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if the non-consummated wife accepts the khul' while she is menstruating?
A) The divorce falls immediately.
B) Nothing falls until she becomes pure from menses.
C) The divorce falls but the payment is deferred.
D) The khul' is void.

Key Takeaway
For a non-consummated wife, khul' with multiple divorces results in one immediate divorce with proportional payment, and remaining divorces are suspended until remarriage.

intermediate
10 min
General Principles of Khul': Yamin and Mubadala
General principles of khul' and linguistic distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the dual nature of khul' as an oath (yamin) from the husband and a financial exchange (mubadala) from the wife, and apply its legal consequences to scenarios involving revocation, suspension, and acceptance.

Core Idea
Khul' is an oath from the husband and a sale from the wife – different rules apply to each side.

The passage continues by outlining the foundational principles of divorce for compensation (khul'). In Hanafi jurisprudence, khul' is not a simple contract but has a dual character: from the husband's perspective it is a binding oath (yamin), from the wife's it is a financial exchange (mubadala). This distinction has practical implications for revocation, suspension, and the effect of standing up from the session. This lesson unpacks these general rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dual nature
Recognize that khul' is treated as a yamin (oath) from the husband's side – his pronouncement is binding like a vow. From the wife's side, it is a mubadala (exchange) – she is buying her release.
This distinction is unique to Hanafi fiqh.
2
Apply the husband's side rules
Because it's a yamin, the husband can add idafa (future time attribution) and ta'liq (suspension on a condition). He cannot revoke his pronouncement once made, and it does not become void if he leaves the session.
His statement is final like an oath.
3
Apply the wife's side rules
Because it's a mubadala, the wife cannot suspend or attribute the divorce to a future time. She may revoke her offer before the husband's acceptance if she initiated. Her standing up from the session invalidates her offer.
These rules mirror those of sale.
4
Analyze the linguistic distinction
The passage distinguishes between 'على أن تعطيني' (on condition that you give) and 'إن أعطيتني' (if you give). In the first, the condition is on acceptance; in the second, on the act of giving. This affects when the divorce falls.
The Quranic verse 9:29 is used to support the interpretation of 'عن يد' as 'by acceptance'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المحيط : لو قال لغير المدخولة أنت طالق ثلاثاً للسنة بألف أو على ألف ولا نية له طلقت واحدة بثلث الألف لأن جميع الأوقات في حق غير المدخولة وقت لطلاق السنة وقد قابل الألف بالثلاث فيتوزع عليها، فإن ويبطل بقيامها ومثل قوله علي ألف على أن تعطيني ألفاً بخلاف إذا اعطيتني أو إذا أجبتني بألف فلا تطلق حتى تعطيه للتصريح بجعل الإعطاء شرطاً بخلافه مع على حتى إنه إذا كان على الزوج دين لها وقعت المقاصة في مسألة على أن تعطيني دون أن أعطيتني إلا أن يرضى الزوج طلاقاً مستقبلاً بألف له عليها، وذلك لأنه يقال على أن تعطيني كذا ويراد قبوله في العرف قال تعالى حتى يعطوا الجزية عن يد وهم صاغرون [التوبة : ۲۹] أي حتى يقبلوا للإجماع على أن بقبولها قوله : (مع أن والفعل بمعنى المصدر) قال الرملي أقول قال في الخانية في الفرق : لأن كلمة «على» لتعليق الايجاب بالقبول لا للتعليق بوجود المقبول ا.هـ. فيتعجب من كلامه وقد تبعه أخوه في ذلك والله تعالى هو الموفق تأمل ا هـ . قلت : لا يخفى عليك أنه لا عجب في كلامهما بل في كلامه لأن ما ذكره لا يصلح فرقاً بين على دخولك وعلى أن تدخلي، والفرق المذكور قد مر في كلام المؤلف عند قوله «ولزمها المال» ثم أعاده قبيل قوله قالت طلقني ثلاثاً بالألف وقد رأيت بخط بعض العلماء نقلاً . السبكي ما يتضح به الفرق إن شاء الله تعالى ونصه : الفرق بين المصدر الصريح «وأن» والفعل المؤلين به اشتراكهما في الدلالة على الحدث أن موضوع صريح المصدر الحدث فقط وهو أمر تصوري، و «أن» والفعل يزيد على ذلك بالحصول إما ماضياً وإما حالاً وإما مستقبلاً إن كان إثباتاً، وبعدم الحصول في ذلك إن كان منفياً In al-Muhit: If he said to a non-consummated wife, 'You are divorced three times for the sunna for a thousand or upon a thousand,' and he had no intention, she is divorced once with one-third of the thousand because all times with respect to a non-consummated woman are times for sunna divorce, and he has matched the thousand with the three, so it is divided among them. If he marries her a second time, she is divorced another with one-third, and likewise a third time, because the pronouncement was valid and does not cease with the termination of ownership; when ownership exists, the condition exists, so it falls and does not require new acceptance from her because acceptance is required in the session of address and it has occurred, except that the falling is delayed due to lack of place, like if he said 'You are divorced tomorrow for a thousand' and she accepted, then tomorrow came, she is divorced for a thousand without acceptance. If she was consummated, one falls in a tuhr in which he did not have intercourse with her with one-third of the thousand, then another in the second tuhr, and another in the third without anything because the compensation is owed in exchange for the ownership of marriage, which has ceased with the first, so she does not own herself for the second for compensation to be valid. If she accepted while she was in a state of menstruation, nothing falls until she menstruates and becomes pure, then it falls as we mentioned. Then know that divorce on property is a yamin from his side, so its idafa and ta'liq are valid, his revocation is not valid, and it is not invalidated by his standing up from the session, and it depends on reaching her if she is absent. From her side it is exchange (mubadala), so her ta'liq and idafa are not valid, and her revocation before the husband's acceptance is valid if she initiated, and it is invalidated by her standing up. An example is his statement 'I have a thousand upon you on condition that you give me a thousand' as opposed to 'if you give me' or 'if you answer me with a thousand' — then she is not divorced until she gives because of the explicit conditioning of giving, contrary to the use of 'ala' [upon condition]. Even if the husband had a debt to her, setoff occurs in the case of 'ala an tu'tini' [on condition that you give me] but not in 'in a'taytini' [if you give me] unless the husband consents to a future divorce for a thousand owed to him. This is because it is said 'ala an tu'tini kaza' and its acceptance is intended in custom. Allah said: 'until they give jizya out of hand while they are subdued' meaning until they accept. Based on consensus that by her acceptance... His statement: 'with 'an' and the verb being in the meaning of the verbal noun.' Al-Ramli said: I say that al-Khaniyya said in the distinction: because the word 'ala' is for conditioning the offer on acceptance, not for conditioning on the existence of the thing accepted. This is astonishing. His brother followed him in that. May Allah grant success. Reflect. I say: It is not hidden from you that there is no astonishment in their words, but rather in his, because what he mentioned does not suffice as a distinction between 'ala dukhulik' and 'ala an tadkhuli'. The distinction mentioned has already passed in the author's words at his statement 'and the money becomes binding on her' and he repeated it before his statement 'she said divorce me three for a thousand.' I have seen in the handwriting of some scholars, quoting al-Subki, what clarifies the distinction, Allah willing. Its text: 'The difference between the explicit verbal noun and 'an' + verb is that they share the indication of the event, but the subject of the explicit verbal noun is the event only, which is a conceptual matter, while 'an' + verb adds to that the occurrence — either past, present, or future if affirmative, and non-occurrence if negative — which is a verititive matter. This is why 'an'+verb can take the place of two objects because of the relationship between them.' End quote. Similar is in al-Ashbah al-Nahwiyyah. You have learned from what has passed that the word 'ala' is a condition and that divorce...
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage covers multiple topics. First, the scenario for a non-consummated wife: the husband says 'أنت طالق ثلاثاً للسنة بألف' without specifying intention. The ruling: one divorce occurs immediately with one-third of the thousand. The remaining two divorces occur if he remarries her and she becomes his wife again, because the original pronouncement was valid but its effect was postponed due to lack of ownership (since after the first divorce, she is not his wife). This is analogous to a suspended divorce ('أنت طالق غداً بألف'). For a consummated wife, one divorce falls in each tuhr, with payment only for the first. Then the author states general principles: divorce for property (khul') is a yamin (oath) from the husband's side, meaning his pronouncement is like a vow — he can add conditions (idafa, ta'liq), cannot revoke it, and it doesn't become void by leaving the session. From the wife's side, it is a financial exchange (mubadala) — she cannot suspend it or add conditions, and she may revoke before the husband's acceptance if she initiated. The text discusses the difference between 'على أن تعطيني' (on condition that you give) and 'إن أعطيتني' (if you give). In the former, acceptance is required; in the latter, it is a condition. The verse from Quran (9:29) is cited as proof for the use of 'عن يد' meaning 'by acceptance.' The final part is a grammatical-legal discussion about the difference between the مصدر صريح (explicit verbal noun) and 'أن + فعل', citing al-Subki. This distinction matters in understanding the difference between 'على دخولك' and 'على أن تدخلي' in khul' contracts. The author of al-Bahr al-Raiq (Ibn Nujaym) refutes a claim by al-Ramli and supports the earlier distinction given by the author.

Key Terms
يمين (yamīn): From the husband's side, the pronouncement of khul' is treated like an oath; it is binding and cannot be revoked.
مبادلة (mubādalah): A financial exchange; from the wife's perspective, khul' is a transaction where she buys her freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The khul' becomes void if the wife has not yet accepted.
B) The husband cannot revoke; his pronouncement is binding like an oath.
C) The revocation is valid only if the wife consents.
D) The revocation is valid if done before leaving the session.

Key Takeaway
Khul' has a dual nature: the husband's side is an unbreakable oath (yamin), the wife's side is a revocable offer (mubadala).

Khul' for Non-Consummated and Consummated Wives
Ruling with proof on khul' distribution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the application of khul' for non-consummated and consummated wives and state the number of divorces and compensation due in each case.

Core Idea
For a non-consummated wife, a triple khul' is distributed over three separate marriages; for a consummated wife, only one divorce is compensated.

In Hanafi fiqh, khul' (divorce by compensation) operates differently depending on whether the marriage has been consummated. This distinction affects how many divorces take effect and how the compensation is distributed. The following passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains these nuances and illustrates the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the wife's status
Determine whether the wife is ghayr madkhulah (non-consummated) or madkhulah (consummated). This is the first determinant of the ruling.
A non-consummated wife can be divorced at any time; a consummated wife can only be divorced in a tuhr (pure period) without intercourse.
2
Parse the husband's pronouncement
Examine the wording: 'You are divorced three times for a thousand dirhams.' The compensation is explicitly tied to the number of divorces.
If no intention is specified, the thousand is distributed evenly across the three divorces.
3
Apply the rule to a non-consummated wife
She is divorced once immediately, with one-third of the thousand due. If he remarries her, another divorce occurs with another third, and a third divorce upon re-remarriage. No new acceptance is needed because the original acceptance suffices.
This is analogous to saying 'You are divorced tomorrow' – acceptance today binds the future event.
4
Apply the rule to a consummated wife
She is divorced once in the first tuhr with one-third of the thousand. A second divorce occurs in the next tuhr with no compensation, and a third in the third tuhr with no compensation. The compensation is only for the first divorce because the marriage bond ends with it.
Compensation is owed in exchange for the marriage ownership, which ceases after the first divorce.
5
Handle the menstruation scenario
If the wife accepts during menstruation, no divorce occurs until she menstruates and becomes pure, then the ruling applies as above.
This applies to consummated wives; for non-consummated, menstruation does not affect the timing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قبلت الثلاث لم تطلق لأنه علق الطلاق بقبولها الألف بإزاء الأربع اهـ. وفي المحيط : لو قال لغير المدخولة أنت طالق ثلاثاً للسنة بألف أو على ألف ولا نية له طلقت واحدة بثلث الألف لأن جميع الأوقات في حق غير المدخولة وقت لطلاق السنة وقد قابل الألف بالثلاث فيتوزع عليها، فإن تزوجها ثانياً طلقت أخرى بثلث الألف، وكذلك ثالثاً لأن الإيقاع كان صحيحاً فلا يرتفع بزوال الملك، فإذا وجد الملك، وجد الشرط فوقع ولا يحتاج إلى قبول جديد منها لأن القبول يشترط في مجلس الخطاب وقد وجد إلا أن الوقوع تأخر لعدم المحل كما لو قال أنت طالق غداً بألف فقبلت فجاء غد طلقت بألف من غير قبول، وإن كانت مدخولة وقعت واحدة في طهر لم يجامعها فيه بثلث الألف ثم أخرى في الطهر الثاني وأخرى في الثالث بغير شيء لأن البدل يجب مقابلاً بملك النكاح وقد زال بالأولى فلا تملك نفسها بالثانية ليصح شيء الاعتياض عنها، وإن قبلت وهي مجامعة لم يقع شيء حتى تحيض وتطهر فيقع حينئذ كما ذكرنا اهـ.
If she accepted three [divorces], she is not divorced because he conditioned the divorce on her acceptance, the thousand being in exchange for four. End. And in al-Muhit: If he said to a non-consummated wife, 'You are divorced three times according to the sunnah for a thousand, or for a thousand,' with no intention, she is divorced once for one-third of the thousand because all times in the case of a non-consummated wife are times for sunnah divorce, and he matched the thousand with the three, so it is distributed over them. Then if he marries her a second time, she is divorced another time for one-third of the thousand, and likewise a third time because the pronouncement was valid, so it is not nullified by the removal of ownership. When ownership exists, the condition exists, so it occurs, and there is no need for new acceptance from her because acceptance is required in the session of address and it has occurred, except that the occurrence was delayed due to the lack of a locus, as if he said, 'You are divorced tomorrow for a thousand,' and she accepted, then tomorrow came, she is divorced for a thousand without acceptance. And if she was consummated, one divorce occurs in a purity in which he did not have intercourse with her for one-third of the thousand, then another in the second purity, and another in the third for nothing because the compensation is owed in exchange for ownership of marriage, and it has been removed by the first, so she does not own herself by the second for any compensation from her to be valid. And if she accepted while she was menstruating, nothing occurs until she menstruates and becomes pure, then it occurs then as we mentioned.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a set of rulings on khul' (divorce by compensation), distinguishing between a non-consummated wife (ghayr madkhulah) and a consummated wife (madkhulah). The author begins by stating that if a husband says to his non-consummated wife 'You are divorced three times for a thousand' and she accepts, she does not get divorced three times because the compensation is in exchange for four (the three divorces plus the cancellation of the deferred dower? Actually 'الألف بإزاء الأربع' means the thousand is in exchange for four things: the three divorces and the dower? The text says 'الألف بإزاء الأربع' which is a reference to a previous discussion. Then it quotes from al-Muhit: If a man says to his non-consummated wife 'You are divorced three times for a thousand' without specifying, she is divorced once for one-third of the thousand. This is because all times are suitable for sunnah divorce in a non-consummated wife, so the thousand is distributed over the three divorces. If he remarries her later, she gets another divorce for another third, and if he remarries again, a third divorce for the remaining third. The reason is that the original pronouncement was valid and does not cease with the ending of marriage; when marriage is renewed, the condition (acceptance) is already fulfilled, so the divorce occurs without new acceptance. This is analogous to divorcing her tomorrow: if she accepts today, the divorce occurs tomorrow without fresh acceptance. For a consummated wife, only one divorce occurs in a pure period, then another in the next period, and a third in the third period, but without compensation after the first because the compensation is owed in exchange for the marriage bond, which ends with the first divorce; thus she cannot be subject to compensation for later divorces. If she accepts while menstruating, no divorce occurs until she menstruates and becomes pure, then it occurs.

Key Terms
خلع (khul'): Divorce initiated by the wife in exchange for compensation paid to the husband.
غير مدخولة (ghayr madkhulah): A wife with whom the husband has not consummated the marriage.
مدخولة (madkhulah): A wife with whom the husband has consummated the marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says to his non-consummated wife, 'You are divorced three times for 1000 dirhams,' and she accepts. How many divorces occur initially, and what compensation is due?
A) Three divorces immediately, and she owes 1000 dirhams.
B) One divorce immediately, and she owes 333.33 dirhams.
C) One divorce immediately, and she owes nothing.
D) No divorce until she pays 1000 dirhams.

Key Takeaway
In khul', the number of divorces and compensation depend on whether the wife is consummated, with non-consummated wives having divorces spread over multiple marriages and consummated wives only compensating the first divorce.

Talaq — Divorce

The Dual Nature of Khul': Husband's Vow and Wife's Exchange
Sets out the legal nature of khul' as a vow from the husband and an exchange from the wife.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the legal nature of khul' from the husband's side (a vow) and from the wife's side (an exchange), and apply the corresponding rules regarding conditions, retraction, and session termination.

Core Idea
Khul' is a vow from the husband, binding and conditional; an exchange from the wife, revocable and unconditional.

Khul' (divorce for compensation) is not a simple divorce. It has two faces: from the husband's perspective, his pronouncement is a vow (yamin), binding and can be made conditional. From the wife's perspective, her offer is an exchange (mubadala) – a purchase of freedom. This fundamental difference dictates distinct rules for each party. Understanding this duality prevents common mistakes in applying khul' rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sides of khul'
Recognize that the husband's role is a vow-maker (yamin) and the wife's role is an exchanger (mubadala). This shapes all subsequent rules.
2
Enumerate the husband's rules as a vow
From the husband's side: he can validly add conditions (ta'liq) and future stipulations, cannot retract his pronouncement, his standing up does not invalidate, and the pronouncement must reach an absent wife.
These follow the nature of oaths.
3
Enumerate the wife's rules as an exchange
From the wife's side: she cannot make her offer conditional or future, she can retract before the husband accepts, and her standing up invalidates her offer.
These follow the nature of contracts of exchange.
4
Apply the linguistic distinction
Analyze the difference between 'ala anna (على أن) and idha (إذا). The first is understood as seeking acceptance (so divorce upon acceptance, not payment); the second makes payment a condition (so no divorce until payment). The Quranic verse (9:29) supports this customary interpretation.
The phrase 'ala anna tu'tini is customarily for acceptance, not actual delivery.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن الطلاق على مال يمين من جهته فتصح إضافته وتعليقة ولا يصح رجوعه ولا يبطل بقيامه عن المجلس، ويتوقف على البلوغ إليها إذا كانت غائبة ومن جهتها مبادلة فلا يصح تعليقها ولا إضافتها، ويصح رجوعها قبل قبول الزوج لو ابتدأت ويبطل بقيامها ومثل قوله علي ألف على أن تعطيني ألفاً بخلاف إذا اعطيتني أو إذا أجبتني بألف فلا تطلق حتى تعطيه للتصريح بجعل الإعطاء شرطاً بخلافه مع على حتى إنه إذا كان على الزوج دين لها وقعت المقاصة في مسألة على أن تعطيني دون أن أعطيتني إلا أن يرضى الزوج طلاقاً مستقبلاً بألف له عليها، وذلك لأنه يقال على أن تعطيني كذا ويراد قبوله في العرف قال تعالى حتى يعطوا الجزية عن يد وهم صاغرون [التوبة : ۲۹] أي حتى يقبلوا للإجماع على أن بقبولها
Then know that divorce for compensation is a vow from his side, so making it conditional and adding a condition is valid, but retracting it is not valid, and it is not invalidated by his standing up from the session. It depends on the pronouncement reaching her if she is absent. From her side, it is an exchange, so making it conditional or adding a condition from her side is not valid, and her retraction is valid before the husband's acceptance if she initiated it, and it is invalidated by her standing up. An example is his saying 'I owe a thousand on condition that you give me a thousand,' unlike if he said 'if you give me' or 'if you respond with a thousand,' then she is not divorced until she gives, because he explicitly made the giving a condition, unlike with 'on condition that'. Even if the husband has a debt owed to her, a set-off occurs in the case of 'on condition that you give me' but not 'if you give me', unless the husband agrees to a future divorce for a thousand owed to him. This is because it is said 'on condition that you give me such and such' and what is intended in custom is acceptance, as God said, 'until they give the jizya with a willing hand' (9:29), meaning until they accept, by consensus that acceptance is intended.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage defines the dual nature of khul': from the husband's side it is considered a vow (yamin), meaning he is making a conditional pronouncement of divorce. Therefore, he can validly add conditions or make it future (ta'liq), he cannot retract once it is pronounced, and it is not invalidated by him leaving the session (majlis). However, it must reach the wife if she is absent. From the wife's side, it is an exchange (mubadala), a purchase of her freedom. Consequently, she cannot make it conditional or future; she can retract before the husband accepts if she initiates; and her standing up invalidates her offer. The author then illustrates with linguistic distinctions: if the husband says 'I owe a thousand on condition that you give me a thousand' (علي ألف على أن تعطيني ألفا), the phrase 'على أن' makes the divorce conditional upon acceptance, not upon actual giving. Compare this to 'if you give me' (إذا أعطيتني) or 'if you respond with a thousand' (إذا أجبتني بألف), where the explicit conditional word 'إذا' makes the giving itself the condition, so she is not divorced until she actually gives. An interesting consequence: if the husband already owes her a debt, a set-off (muqasa) occurs in the 'على أن' case (since acceptance is what matters) but not in the 'إذا' case, unless the husband agrees to a future divorce for the debt. The custom (urf) supports this: the phrase 'على أن تعطيني' is understood as seeking acceptance, not actual delivery. The author supports this with the Quranic verse 'until they give the jizya' (9:29), where 'give' is interpreted by consensus as 'accept' because the giving is in the future.

Key Terms
يمين (yamin): A vow or oath; the husband's pronouncement of khul' has the legal force of a vow, making it binding once uttered.
مبادلة (mubadala): An exchange or barter; from the wife's perspective, khul' is a purchase of her freedom from the marriage bond.
قبول (qabul): Acceptance; the wife's acceptance of the khul' offer is the moment when the exchange is concluded.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi law, is it valid for a wife to say, 'I accept the khul' on condition that you pay me later'?
A) Yes, because she can add conditions.
B) No, because from her side it is an exchange and cannot be conditional.
C) Yes, but only with the husband's consent.
D) No, because only the husband can add conditions.

Key Takeaway
Khul' is a hybrid transaction: a binding vow from the husband, a revocable exchange from the wife.

Talaq — Divorce

Distinction between 'على أن تعطيني' and 'على أن تدخلي' in Khul'
Explains the linguistic-legal difference between conditional phrases in khul' and the rule for multiple divorces with lump sum compensation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between conditional phrases in khul' and apply the rule for apportioning compensation when multiple divorces are mentioned.

Core Idea
‘على أن تعطيني’ requires acceptance as a conditional exchange; ‘على أن تدخلي’ is a pure condition on future action.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq navigates the subtle linguistic-legal distinctions in khul' conditions, building on earlier works like al-Khaniya. The author clarifies when a wife's acceptance is needed and when it is not, and also provides a rule for dividing compensation when two divorces are mentioned with a single amount. Mastering these nuances is essential for correctly applying Hanafi law in divorce cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three key phrases
Recognize the three phrases discussed: 'على أن تعطيني' (on condition that you give me), 'على أن تدخلي' (on condition that you enter), and 'على دخولك الدار' (on your entering the house).
The first two use 'أن' + verb, the third uses an explicit masdar.
2
Classify each phrase as exchange or pure condition
For 'على أن تعطيني', the verb implies a transfer of wealth, so it is treated as an exchange (mu'awada) requiring acceptance. For 'على أن تدخلي', it is a pure condition on a future action, so no acceptance is needed.
The presence of compensation (mal) triggers the exchange classification.
3
Analyze the explicit masdar phrase
For 'على دخولك', the explicit masdar 'دخول' is treated like a compensation term due to linguistic custom (urf). The divorce depends on acceptance, not actual entry, to avoid temporal ambiguity.
This is based on the fact that the masdar sarih only denotes the event, not the time.
4
Apply the rule for multiple divorces with a lump sum
When two divorces are mentioned together with a single amount of compensation, the money is equally divided between them unless the first divorce is described as irrevocable or otherwise preventing the obligation of money.
If the first is irrevocable, the entire compensation goes to the second.
5
Work through examples
Use the author's examples: 'You are divorced now one and tomorrow another for a thousand' – 500 for each; if he remarries before tomorrow, the second divorce occurs and she owes 500.
The key is to identify the timing of divorces and the nature of the condition.

With 'anna' and the verb in the sense of the masdar, al-Ramli said: I say, it was said in al-Khaniya regarding the difference: because the word 'ala is for linking the offer to acceptance, not for linking to the existence of the accepted thing. End. So he marvels at his statement, and his brother followed him in that, and Allah is the one who grants success. Ponder. End. The mentioned difference has already passed in the author's speech at his statement 'and the money is incumbent upon her,' then he repeated it before his statement 'she said: divorce me three times for a thousand.' And I have seen in the handwriting of some scholars, quoting al-Subki, what clarifies the difference, if Allah wills. Its text: The difference between the explicit masdar and 'anna with the verb derived from it is that they share in indicating the event, but the subject of the explicit masdar is the event only, which is a conceptual matter. 'Anna with the verb adds to that the attainment, either past, present, or future if affirmative, or non-attainment if negative, which is a declarative matter. For this reason, 'anna with the verb occupies the position of two objects due to what is between them of the relative connection. End verbatim. Similar is in al-Ashbah al-Nahwiyya. And you have known from what has passed that the word 'ala is a condition and that divorce [PAGE_140] 'anna, but between 'anna and 'idha and 'mata' there is a difference: in 'anna, the divorce depends on the giving in the session, unlike 'idha and 'mata'. And in Jawami' al-Fiqh: He said to a non-related woman, 'You are divorced for a thousand if I marry you,' and she accepted, then he married her; the acceptance is not considered except after the marriage because it is a khul' after marriage, so acceptance is required after. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. And if he said, 'Because it is divorce for compensation after marriage,' it would be more appropriate. And I was asked at the Sarghatmish school about the difference between 'ala an tu'teeni where it depends on acceptance and 'ala an tadkhuli al-dar where it depends on entry, and also the difference between 'anti taliq 'ala dukhulik al-dar where it depends on her acceptance, not on entry, as in al-Khaniya, and 'ala an tadkhuli where acceptance is insufficient, with 'anna and the verb being in the sense of the masdar. And here there is a rule regarding divorce for compensation: the principle is that whenever two divorces are mentioned and money is mentioned after them, it is in exchange for both, since it is not more appropriate to direct the compensation to one rather than the other, unless the first is described with something that contradicts the obligation of money, in which case the money is then in exchange for the second. Describing it as contradictory is like explicitly stating that the money is in exchange for the second. And if money is made a condition, it is obligatory upon the woman to obtain separation, because she only obliges herself to acquire ownership of herself. So if he said to her, 'You are divorced now one, and tomorrow another, for a thousand,' or said 'on condition that you are divorced tomorrow another for a thousand,' or said 'today one, and tomorrow another revocable for a thousand,' and she accepts, one divorce occurs for five hundred now, and tomorrow another for nothing, unless his ownership returns before it, because he combined an immediate divorce and a future one and mentioned money after them, so it applies to both. Do you not see that if he mentioned an exception instead of the compensation, it would apply to both, so today one for five hundred, and when tomorrow comes, another occurs due to the existence of the time added to it, and nothing is owed because the condition for obligation (the attainment of separation) by the second divorce did not occur, as it occurred by the first. Even if he married her before the arrival of tomorrow, then tomorrow comes, another occurs for five hundred due to the existence of the condition for obligation of money. And if he said, 'You are divorced now one, the money in exchange for a compensation from her side,' then her acceptance is required. When this is clear, we say: If he said to her, 'on condition that you give me,' he has made her divorce conditional on her giving him money in the future, so it is a compensation, so her acceptance is required. Thus it becomes as if he conditioned it on acceptance because by it his purpose of divorcing for compensation is achieved, as it becomes binding upon her by acceptance. As for his saying, 'on condition that you enter,' there is no compensation in it, so it remains on its original rule: conditional on future entry, and no debt is incurred by her, so her acceptance is not required, nor is it linked to it. As for his saying, 'on your entry into the house,' entry has been used in the manner of compensations, so the condition is acceptance of the compensation, not its existence, as if he said 'on condition that you give me a thousand,' as in the chapter on ta'liq from al-Muhit near his statement 'so in it, if the condition exists, the vow ends.' It is used thus because if it were conditional on entry as in the previous issue, it would necessitate changing the subject of the masdar, since entry must be intended as past, present, or future, and the explicit masdar is a subject for the event itself, and there is uncertainty regarding the time of the condition. Therefore it is used in the manner of compensations, so it depends on acceptance. This is what appears to me, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This extensive passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq delves into a subtle linguistic-legal distinction crucial for khul' rulings. The issue arises when different phrasings are used: 'على أن تعطيني' (on condition that you give me), 'على أن تدخلي' (on condition that you enter), 'على دخولك الدار' (on your entering the house). The author navigates various opinions, including those from al-Khaniya, al-Ramli, al-Subki, and others, to establish the Hanafi position.

The core distinction: In 'على أن تعطيني', the word 'على' is understood as linking the offer (the husband's divorce pronouncement) to the wife's acceptance, not to the actual act of giving. Therefore, the divorce is conditional upon acceptance in the same session. Once she accepts, the divorce occurs immediately, and she owes the compensation (it becomes a debt). This is because the phrase is treated as an exchange (mu'awada), and acceptance is what completes the exchange.

In contrast, 'على أن تدخلي' conditions the divorce on the future act of entering (the house). Here, there is no exchange; it is a pure conditional vow (ta'liq). The divorce only occurs when the entry actually happens, not upon acceptance. Hence, no acceptance is required from the wife; the condition is the action itself.

The phrase 'على دخولك الدار' is interesting: it uses the explicit masdar (verbal noun) 'دخول'. The author explains that in this construction, the masdar is used in the manner of compensations, meaning it functions like 'على أن تعطيني'. So the divorce is conditional on acceptance, not on actual entry. This is because the explicit masdar, as al-Subki notes, signifies only the event (abstract concept), lacking tense. Using it conditionally would create uncertainty about the time, so custom (urf) redirects it to acceptance.

The passage also provides a general rule: when two divorces are mentioned together followed by a lump sum compensation, the money is apportioned equally between them, unless the first divorce is described in a way that prevents obligation of money (e.g., if the first is an irrevocable divorce that ends the marriage, the second cannot be valid). In that case, all money goes to the second. Examples: if he says 'You are divorced one now and another tomorrow for a thousand,' the compensation is split: five hundred for the immediate divorce, and five hundred for the future one, but the future one does not occur if the marriage has already ended (by the first divorce). If he remarries her before the future time, then the second divorce occurs and she owes the remaining five hundred.

Finally, the author summarizes: if he says 'على أن تعطيني', it is a conditional exchange requiring acceptance; if he says 'على أن تدخلي', it is a pure condition without exchange, no acceptance needed; if he says 'على دخولك', it is treated like the first due to linguistic custom, so acceptance is required. This nuanced rule shows the Hanafi commitment to both linguistic analysis and customary understanding.

Key Terms
على ('ala): A preposition used to create a conditional clause; its legal meaning depends on context — sometimes conditioning on acceptance, sometimes on action.
مصدر صريح (masdar sarih): An explicit verbal noun that denotes the event without reference to time.
تعليق (ta'liq): Suspending the occurrence of an act (here, divorce) upon a future condition.
معاوضة (mu'awada): A mutual exchange; here, a divorce that is conditional on the wife providing compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says to his wife: 'You are divorced on condition that you give me 500 dirhams.' She immediately says 'I accept.' When does the divorce take effect?
A) Immediately upon acceptance.
B) Only when she actually gives the 500 dirhams.
C) It does not take effect because the condition is invalid.
D) It takes effect only if she gives the money in the same session.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between conditional exchange requiring acceptance and pure condition on future action based on linguistic custom and the presence of compensation; the explicit masdar is always treated as compensation.

Distinction Between Forms of Khul' and the Base Rule
Explaining the difference in requiring acceptance between three conditional khul' formulas and presenting a base rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the three khul' formulas regarding the requirement of acceptance and apply the Hanafi base rule for allocating a single sum of money across multiple divorces.

Core Idea
In khul', acceptance is required only when a financial exchange (mu'awada) is involved; the base rule splits a single sum equally between two divorces unless the first is described as free of obligation.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a practical distinction between three conditional divorce formulas that involve payment or conditions, and then lays down a general rule for cases where a husband pronounces two divorces with a single sum of money. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correctly determining when the wife's acceptance is needed and how to allocate payment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three formulas
The author distinguishes 'ala an tu'tini (on condition that you give me), 'ala an tadkhuli (on condition that you enter), and 'ala dukhuliki al-dar (on your entering the house). Note which require acceptance and which do not.
Focus on the presence or absence of a financial exchange.
2
Understand why acceptance is required for the first formula
'Ala an tu'tini' creates a mu'awada (exchange) similar to a sale. The divorce is conditional upon the wife giving money, and her acceptance makes the exchange binding.
The divorce occurs as ba'in upon acceptance.
3
Understand why acceptance is not required for the second formula
'Ala an tadkhuli' has no financial element; it is a pure ta'liq on an act. The divorce is suspended on entry, not on acceptance.
The wife's acceptance is irrelevant.
4
Analyze the third formula as an exception
'Ala dukhuliki al-dar' uses a masdar, but due to temporal ambiguity, it is treated as an exchange (istihsan). Thus, acceptance is required, not the actual entry.
This is a departure from the literal meaning to avoid impracticality.
5
Apply the base rule for multiple divorces with a single sum
When two divorces are mentioned and a sum follows, it is divided equally unless the first is described as free of financial obligation. Walk through the provided examples.
The sum is allocated to both divorces equally by default.

يتعلق به وأما قوله على دخولك الدار فقد استعمل فيه الدخول استعمال الأعواض فكان الشرط قبول العوض لا وجوده كما لو قال «على أن تعطيني ألفاً» كما في باب التعليق عن المحيط قبيل قوله ففيها إن وجد الشرط انتهت المعلق عليه باعتبار الزمان فلذا استعمل استعمال الأعواض فتعلق على القبول. هذا ما ظهر لي والله تعالى أعلم .
And it was asked of me in the Sarghatmishiyya school the difference between 'on condition that you give me' where it is conditioned on acceptance, and 'on condition that you enter the house' where it is conditioned on entering, and also asked the difference between 'you are divorced on your entering the house' where it is conditioned on her acceptance not on entering as in al-Khaniyya, and 'on condition that you enter' where acceptance is not sufficient despite 'in' and the verb having the meaning of the masdar. And here is a base rule in divorce for money: the principle is that whenever two divorces are mentioned and a sum of money is mentioned after them, it is in exchange for both, since one is not more entitled to receive the payment than the other, unless the first is described with something that contradicts the obligation of payment, in which case the payment is then in exchange for the second, and describing it with a contradiction is like specifying that the payment is in exchange for the second. And even if the money is made a condition, it becomes obligatory upon the woman only upon the occurrence of separation, because she is only obliged to purchase herself. So if he said to her: 'You are divorced now once, and tomorrow another, for one thousand,' or he said: 'On condition that you are divorced tomorrow another for one thousand,' or he said: 'Today one, and tomorrow another revocable for one thousand,' and she accepted, one divorce occurs immediately for five hundred, and tomorrow another without anything, unless he resumes marital rights before that, because he combined an immediate divorce and a deferred divorce and mentioned money after both so it is allocated to both. Do you not see that if he had mentioned an exception instead of the payment, it would apply to both, so today one occurs for five hundred, and when tomorrow comes another occurs due to the existence of the time to which it was attached, and nothing is owed because the condition for the obligation of payment was the occurrence of separation by the second divorce, which did not occur because separation already occurred by the first. So even if he marries her before tomorrow comes, then tomorrow comes, another occurs for five hundred due to the existence of the condition for the obligation of money. And if he said: 'You are divorced now once, the money is in exchange for a consideration from her side, so her acceptance is required.' When that is clear, we say: if he said to her 'on condition that you give me,' he has suspended her divorce on her giving him money in the future, so it is a mu'awada (exchange) thus her acceptance is required, so it becomes as if he suspended it on acceptance, because by acceptance his objective of divorce for compensation is achieved due to its binding upon her by acceptance. As for his saying 'on condition that you enter,' there is no exchange in it, so it remains on its original basis of being suspended on entry in the future, and no financial liability attaches to her, so her acceptance is not required nor is it attached to it. As for his saying 'on your entering the house,' entering has been used here as a substitute, so the condition is acceptance of the substitute, not its occurrence, just as if he said 'on condition that you give me a thousand,' as in the chapter of ta'liq from al-Muhit just before his saying 'in it, if the condition occurs, the oath ends.' And it has been used thus because if it were suspended on entry as in the previous case, it would necessitate changing the meaning of the masdar, since it must mean entry in the past, present, or future, and the explicit masdar is placed for the event itself, with the added ambiguity of the time of the suspended matter, therefore it has been used as a substitute and is suspended on acceptance. This is what appeared to me, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is a teacher's explanation of the distinction between three related formulas in khul' divorce, followed by a base rule for multiple divorces with a single payment. The author, writing in a sharh style, first states that he was asked in a madrasa to clarify why acceptance is required in one formula but not another. He explains: (1) 'ala an tu'tini (on condition that you give me): Since the wife is undertaking a financial obligation, the divorce is an exchange (mu'awada) requiring her acceptance; the divorce is effectively suspended on acceptance. (2) 'ala an tadkhuli (on condition that you enter [the house]): There is no financial exchange, so the divorce is suspended only on the act of entering; acceptance is irrelevant. (3) 'ala dukhuliki al-dar (on your entering the house): Although it uses a masdar, in current usage it is treated like an exchange, so the condition is the wife's acceptance of the substitute, not the act itself; this is an istihsan ruling because the masdar's temporal ambiguity makes it unsuitable for direct ta'liq. The author then presents a general qawida: when a man says two divorces (e.g., one now and one tomorrow) and mentions a sum of money after them, the money is divided equally between both divorces unless the first is described in a way that precludes an obligation of money (like saying it is bā'in or without compensation), in which case the entire payment is for the second divorce. He illustrates this with examples, showing how the payment is halved if both divorces are included, and how the second divorce's payment becomes due only if the husband remarries before its occurrence.

Key Terms
على أن تعطيني (ʿalā an tuʿṭīnī): A formula in khul' where the husband says 'on condition that you give me [something]' – considered a mu'awada (exchange) requiring the wife's acceptance.
قاعدة في الطلاق على مال (qāʿidah fī al-ṭalāq ʿalā māl): A legal maxim: when two divorces are mentioned with a single sum, the sum is divided equally unless the first is described as free of financial obligation.
معاوضة (muʿāwaḍa): An exchange transaction; in khul', it means the divorce is treated as a bilateral contract requiring acceptance.
تعليق (taʿlīq): Suspending the occurrence of a divorce on a future condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the phrase 'أنت طالق على أن تدخلي الدار', why is the wife's acceptance not required?
A) Because it is a revocable divorce
B) Because there is no financial exchange involved; it is suspended on an act
C) Because the husband's statement is ambiguous
D) Because the wife must perform the act first

Key Takeaway
In khul', acceptance is only required when the formula explicitly creates a financial exchange (mu'awada); for pure ta'liq formulas, acceptance is irrelevant, and the base rule divides a single sum equally between consecutive divorces unless the first is described as free of obligation.

The Waw in Khul': Conjunctive or Circumstantial?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on waw interpretation in khul'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi positions on the waw in 'Anti taliq wa 'alayki alf', identify the linguistic and usul proofs, and state the practical consequence of each interpretation.

Core Idea
A single letter (waw) determines whether divorce is conditional on payment—literal meaning vs. figurative usage.

In Hanafi fiqh, the phrase 'Anti taliq wa 'alayki alf' (You are divorced and upon you is a thousand) is a classic point of divergence between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two chief disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. The dispute hinges on the nature of the waw: is it a simple conjunctive (atf) or does it indicate a circumstantial clause (hal)? The difference drastically affects whether the wife owes the money and whether the divorce takes effect immediately or only upon acceptance. In this lesson, we unpack the arguments, the Arabic linguistic analysis, and the final tarjih in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
State the two positions
Identify what each side holds regarding the occurrence of divorce and the obligation to pay. Abu Hanifa says divorce occurs without payment regardless of acceptance; the Sahibayn say divorce occurs only if she accepts to pay, and then she owes the money.
Both sides agree on the ruling if the waw is clearly conjunctive; the dispute is only in this specific phrasing.
2
Analyze the linguistic argument
Understand why the Sahibayn say the waw cannot be conjunctive: the first sentence is performative (insha'i) and the second is declarative (khabari), creating a 'disconnection' that forces a figurative reading (hal). Abu Hanifa responds that the first sentence can be declarative (khabari) in meaning, removing the disconnection.
The author cites Fath al-Qadir for this rebuttal.
3
Examine the proofs from Arabic usage
The Sahibayn support their view with Quranic examples like 'Inzilu wa antum aminun' (Descend and you are safe) and the principle of bara'at al-dhimmah (presumption of no liability). They also note that in hiring contracts the waw can indicate exchange.
Abu Hanifa's literal approach is also used in other contexts, but here the preferred view is the Sahibayn's.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
State the practical difference: according to Abu Hanifa, the wife is divorced immediately without any financial obligation; according to the Sahibayn, she is not divorced unless she accepts the payment. This affects maintenance payments, 'iddah, and possibility of reconciliation.
The commentary notes that even the resumptive (isti'naf) interpretation does not impose payment without acceptance.
5
Identify the mufta bihi position
The text indicates that the relied-upon view is that of the Sahibayn, based on the principle of bara'at al-dhimmah and the figurative interpretation of the waw as circumstantial.
The author of al-Bahr al-Raiq leans toward the Sahibayn, as do later Hanafi fatwa works.

His saying: 'You are divorced and upon you is a thousand, or you are free and upon you is a thousand, she is divorced and he is free without payment' means whether she accepts or not according to Imam Abu Hanifa. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it occurs if they accept and the money is binding on them, otherwise not, acting on the interpretation of the waw as circumstantial (hal) figuratively, because it is impossible to take it as conjunctive due to the disconnection, since the first sentence is performative (insha'i) and the second is declarative (khabari). And according to him (Abu Hanifa), the waw is conjunctive here, acting on the literal meaning, and there is no disconnection because in reality the first sentence is declarative, not performative. This is in Fath al-Qadir. And it is mentioned in his Tahrir that the more likely view is that the waw is resumptive (isti'naf), whether number or otherwise, not conjunctive due to disconnection. There is no doubt that it is figurative, but it is preferred over the figurative interpretation of circumstantial on the basis of the original principle, which is the presumption of innocence and not imposing money without a clear basis. And they agreed that it is circumstantial in 'Pay a thousand and you are free' and 'Descend and you are safe' because it is impossible to take it as conjunctive due to the complete disconnection between the two sentences, but it is a case of inversion because the condition is the payment and the descent. And they agreed that it means 'with' (ba') that is exchange in his saying 'Carry this food and you have a dirham' because the conjunctive in exchange is original in hiring. And they agreed on the determination of the original without any other possibility in his saying: 'Except when he describes the first with something that contradicts the obligation...' This exception indicates that his saying 'firstly' means it is allocated to both whether he described none of them with a contradiction, or described both, or described only the second. This is clarified by what is in al-Tatarkhaniyya from al-Muhit: 'And if he said to her: You are divorced now one revocable, and tomorrow another revocable for a thousand, or he said: You are divorced now one bā'in, and tomorrow another bā'in for a thousand dirhams, or he said: You are divorced now one without anything, and tomorrow another for a thousand dirhams, then the payment is allocated to both, and each divorce is for half the thousand, so one occurs immediately for half the thousand, and tomorrow for free unless he marries her before tomorrow comes, then when tomorrow comes another occurs for half the thousand.' End quote. His saying: 'And the more likely view is that the waw is resumptive' meaning for number or otherwise, i.e., dirham.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the interpretation of the conjunction waw in the khul' formula 'Anti taliq wa 'alayki alf' (You are divorced and upon you is a thousand). The author, commenting on a concise statement of the matn, explains the difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). According to Abu Hanifa, the waw is literal conjunctive (atf), so the statement is simply a divorce without any financial obligation; the woman is divorced without payment regardless of acceptance. The Sahibayn, however, interpret the waw as circumstantial (hal), meaning 'being such that upon you is a thousand', so it is a conditional divorce requiring acceptance; if she accepts, she owes the money, otherwise no divorce occurs. The commentary then cites Fath al-Qadir and al-Tahrir, noting that some argue the waw is resumptive (isti'naf) rather than conjunctive or circumstantial, but the preferred view is still the circumstantial interpretation due to the presumption of no liability. The author also discusses other examples from the Quran and common usage to support the interpretations, and clarifies that when the waw is truly conjunctive (like in hiring contracts), it implies exchange. The passage ends with a clarification of the exception mentioned earlier: if the first divorce is described as bā'in or without payment, then the payment is for the second only.

Key Terms
الواو للحال (al-wāw lil-ḥāl): The waw that indicates the circumstantial clause, meaning 'while' or 'being such that'.
الواو للعطف (al-wāw lil-ʿaṭf): The conjunctive waw that simply joins two elements.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) She is divorced and must pay a thousand.
B) She is divorced and owes nothing.
C) No divorce occurs.
D) She is divorced but must pay half.

Key Takeaway
The waw in 'Anti taliq wa 'alayki alf' is interpreted as circumstantial (hal) according to the mufta bihi Hanafi position, so divorce is conditional on the wife's acceptance of the payment.
